B007BRSN72|5.0|This 2012 edition of Younger Than Yesterday is from Sony Japan. Like the other titles in the series (Mr. Tambourine Man; Turn! Turn! Turn!; Fifth Dimension; The Notorious Byrd Brothers), this disc incorporates Blu-Spec manufacturing technology but plays fine on a standard CD player.<br /><br />If you were to ask me if I think this disc offers improved sound over the 1996 Sony remaster, I would say \\"Not that I can hear.\\" But I don't have a high-end sound system. A true audiophile with a bitchin' sound system and an ear for such things might pick up nuances beyond my grasp (and my sound system's specs). If so, I would respect that person's findings. But to me, both this new import and the 1996 edition offer great sound. Yet there are some important distinctions to be made between these two versions released 16 years apart -- especially the first point here:<br /><br />1996 YTY: has just the stereo mix; I had only it to A/B with the 2012 edition's stereo mix; that's because the . . .<br />2012 YTY: has the stereo mix of the album AND its corresponding mono mix; having the mono mix again after all these years is the main reason I bought this import. I should emphasize here that Audio Fidelity released a mono CD of YTY last year, with remastering done by highly respected audio engineer Steve Hoffman. As I do not own a copy of that CD, I don't know how it and this 2012 Sony Japan version compare. (However, the Hoffman mono remaster has garnered quite favorable reviews.)<br /><br />1996 YTY: 6 bonus tracks (all in stereo)<br />2012 YTY: 3 bonus tracks (all in mono)<br /><br />1996 YTY: a 16-page booklet with David Fricke's excellent essay, Johnny Rogan's authoritative song notes, and some cool photos<br />2012 YTY: a 24-page, text-heavy booklet (almost all in Japanese, of course) with track listing and song lyrics in English.  Booklets for the other 2012s contain a few errors in the printed lyrics, but I haven't taken the time to thoroughly read the lyrics in this booklet; maybe they're error-free.<br /><br />1996 YTY: housed in a standard jewel case with a clear tray through which the original back cover photo shows<br />2012 YTY: housed in a beautifully rendered facsimile album jacket about half an inch taller than a standard plastic jewel case; the CD itself mimics a black vinyl record (complete with \\"grooves\\") with a red Sony Music label; it, like the 1996 remaster, has the iconic Columbia Records \\"walking eye\\" logo; the disc itself is snugly protected by a U-shaped sleeve<br /><br />Here's the track order for this new 2012 import edition of Younger Than Yesterday:<br />Tracks 1-11 are the mono mix of the original album.<br />Tracks 12-14 are the mono bonus tracks \\"Don't Make Waves\\" [single version]; \\"It Happens Each Day\\"; and \\"My Back Pages\\" [single version] -- with the second verse omitted, it clocks in at 2:32.<br />Tracks 15-25 are the stereo mix of the original album.<br /><br />Producer Gary Usher's stereo mix has such a wonderful spatial presentation. YTY in stereo is just a great listen -- always has been. It has a majestic quality to it. But decades ago, in the Age of Vinyl, YTY was, for me, the one Byrds album whose mono mix always competed with its stereo sibling for turntable time. Usher's mono mix is solid. And although this must seem counterintuitive to listeners who prefer their music in stereo, I think the mono mix -- at least of specific songs -- actually uncovers some of the instrumentation better than the stereo version does. Or, perhaps it's more accurate to say it aims the spotlight where it needs to be. For example, Clarence White's fabulous picking on \\"Time Between\\" is front and center in its mono mix. You can still hear it in the left channel of the stereo mix, and you can tell it's good, but it's still just part of the song's landscape. Chris Hillman's lead vocals on the song's mono mix also have more presence -- as they should. The percussion in the mono mix is also more conspicuous than it is in the stereo mix and adds a lot to the song's sense of urgency.<br /><br />Similarly, Roger McGuinn's fretwork on \\"So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star\\" packs more punch in the mono mix -- again, because it's front and center. (On the other hand, Hugh Masekela's trumpet is better heard in the stereo version. It's prominent in the right channel while Roger's Ric-12 is prominent in the left.)<br /><br />Another notable mono/stereo difference pops up in \\"C.T.A.-102.\\" That little ascending piano run shortly before the alien voices appear is far more dramatic in the mono version. (In fact, for a moment there I thought I was listening to a passage from the Moody Blues' album, In Search Of The Lost Chord.) The mono mix of \\"The Girl With No Name\\" has a better payoff because, once again, Clarence's guitar fills and solo are front and center, and more Clarence is always a good thing.<br /><br />This is not to say the mono mix is packed with \\"all the good stuff\\" at the expense of the stereo mix. Far from it. In the album's stereo mix, \\"Have You Seen Her Face\\" has a longer fade (by 10 seconds) that's more engaging than the mono mix. The stereo mix of \\"Renaissance Fair\\" has that nifty little guitar fill from Roger just after the first refrain of \\"I think that maybe I'm dreamin'\\" near the end; the mono version doesn't have that -- or if it does it's buried in the mix. And there are some other nice moments in the stereo mix in which a particular instrument is showcased a little more.<br /><br />The upshot of all this is that each mix -- mono and stereo -- on this new 2012 edition has its distinctive elements and its particular joys. I would caution those of you unaccustomed to listening to pop and rock albums presented in mono that it is a very different experience from listening to those same albums in stereo. So please don't rush out and buy this pricey import just because I like both mixes. Remember, I grew up with both. Ultimately, the music itself is what matters most. Younger Than Yesterday remains one of the Byrds' finest works.reo mix and adds a lot to the song's sense of urgency.<br /><br />Similarly, Roger McGuinn's fretwork on \\"So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star\\" packs more punch in the mono mix -- again, because it's front and center. It's prominent in the right channel while Roger's Ric-12 is prominent in the left.)<br /><br />Another notable mono/stereo difference pops up in \\"C.T.A.-102.\\" That little ascending piano run shortly before the alien voices appear is far more dramatic in the mono version. B00466HM28|4.0|At first, I wasn't really sure what to expect with this little keyboard. I had around a hundred dollars to spend on something for my studio, and this seemed like a great option considering it was also portable (I record with friends on the go a lot, and I needed to be able to throw it in my backpack). When I first got it, the keyboard very much impressed me with how it felt, but the pads felt a little stiff. Later, after getting an ASIO capable audio interface and fixing some laptop issues, this thing is amazing. Not only can I control Ableton Live or any other software that accepts MIDI CC signals with this, the keyboard holds up fantastically, it's light, portable, and actually professional looking even next to large mixers and controllers like the APC-40.<br /><br />My only serious complaint is this - the software that is bundled with this unit to change it's MIDI functions is completely unintuitive, and if you don't know MIDI, downright unusable. I figured it out though, and it does work with some tweaking. Also, the pads can't be played with drumsticks (should be obvious, I suppose, but they can't be), so don't go pounding away at them.<br /><br />Overall, this piece of gear has really helped out my studio, especially now that Ableton Live supports it as a native control surface. It's both a great MIDI piano and a great MIDI controller, especially since each function can be mapped to a different MIDI channel. I highly recommend this product.<br /><br />As a sidenote, it works on my Windows 7 PC, an XP box I tried it on, and of course flawlessly on my friend's MacBook Pro. Driver issues seem nonexistant. Very nice, and rare for a such a cheap product.

B008645YEE|5.0|I was extremely excited for this score, and - in short - it's passed my high expectations and manages to ramp up the \\"epicness\\" even more than the Dark Knight's score, which I doubted was possible until hearing this! Zimmer manages to bring old sounds back from the first two scores, which tie things together well (almost too well at times, some parts are copy/paste jobs from previous scores) but he almost always incorporates something to freshen things up!<br /><br />That being said there were a few things I wasn't crazy about on this score. The first and most glaring was the way the chant was mixed into things. I was expecting the chant to be a very prominent part of this score, with variable performances (maybe even some that would border on screaming the chant). Unfortunately not only does the chant get mixed relatively into the background in the tracks it appears, but there's nothing in the way it sounds to even suggest it's any different than the recording we got in the trailers, back before Zimmer's big \\"record your own chant!\\" promotion. That's not to say I don't still LOVE the sounds of the chant and they really add an interesting flair when it appears (not to mention the novelty of the 5/4 time in Bane's music, which is unique and refreshing amongst a lot of current scores).<br /><br />I was also dissappointed by the new themes included. The \\"Catwoman\\" theme, if you want to call it that (they never specifically call her \\"Catwoman\\" in the movie) is interesting and the echoey piano is a nice change from the dark strings, but ultimately the theme doesn't go very far - only appearing on the album once (though the cymbal sounds backing it crop up subtly a few times), and in the film twice. I would have liked to hear a bit more of it, perhaps vary the instrumentation a bit. Finally, the chromatic lines are reminescent of Zimmer's Sherlock scores, not a bad thing but worth noting.<br /><br />While working on The Dark Knight, Zimmer had made a promise that the Batman \\"theme\\" would be expanded upon to it's fullest variation in this final score. He claimed that what we hear in Dark Knight was \\"just a small taste\\" of the grand theme he had in store. However as far as I can tell this expansion is not found anywhere in the score, for any of the major themes for batman (the two note \\"heroic fanfare\\", the Da Vinci Code-esque \\"emotional theme\\", and the darker batman theme heard on low basses/cellos). So I was dissappointed by this lack of expansion. The most we get is the two note horn fanfare gains a few extra notes in \\"Imagine the Fire\\" which is a fantastic track.<br /><br />There is one moment of EXCELLENT thematic development in the score that is only heard on the bonus track \\"No Stone Unturned\\" which can be found with the physical CD as a download. In the last minute of this track we hear the two note horn fanfare changed into a two note cello ostinato, which shifts keys and eventually settles into F major, which is simply fantastic if you're musical enough to notice and appreciate the variation presented there. It can be heard in the beginning of the film when Gordon goes to give his speech about Harvey Dent, and it's wonderful sounding :)<br /><br />----<br /><br />All in all this is a GREAT score by Zimmer, and easily the best of the three Batman Scores. Unlike the Dark Knight which felt like it was missing music in the initial release, if one is able to collect the four bonus tracks available online for this score you really have a fairly complete collection of the music in the film (except perhaps a few tracks for more intimate scenes). Having seen the film and heard the music in it I can say confidently that all major parts of the score are well represented. Also, unlike The Dark Knight this score also has most of the tracks in chronological ordering (with a few exceptions, however some tracks are repeated in the film in a few places) and the way many tracks flow into the next is nice and makes for an enjoyable experience.<br /><br />GET THIS SOUNDTRACK if you enjoy Hans Zimmer's music for the Batman series. If you need to own only one score, get this one over The Dark Knight or Batman Begins, for it's by far the most epic and intense of the three, and just well done all around.<br /><br />If you can manage to collect all of the bonus tracks available, personally I've found this to be a good track listing that puts the tracks in mostly the right order and maintains connected tracks/makes a good listening experience. Feel free to comment if you have other track orderings you like:<br /><br />1. A Storm Is Coming<br />2. On Thin Ice<br />3. Gotham's Reckoning<br />4. Mind If I Cut In?<br />5. Underground Army<br />6. Born In Darkness<br />7. The Fire Rises<br />8. Nothing Out There<br />9. All Out War (Bonus Track from ticket sale)<br />10. Despair<br />11. Fear Will Find You<br />12. Why Do We Fall?<br />13. Death By Exile<br />14. The Shadows Betray You (Mp3 Bonus Track)<br />15. Risen From Darkness (CD Bonus Track)<br />16. Imagine The Fire<br />17. No Stone Unturned (CD Bonus Track)<br />18. Necessary Evil<br />19. The End (Mp3 Bonus Track)<br />20. Rise<br /><br />My final note...the remix tracks at the ends of Zimmer's scores need to stop...nobody's buying this score for a random [not even that good] dubstep song where more score could have been put in. It's not TERRIBLE...but even so...

B004LWZWGK|5.0|Christopher Nolan's triumphant, apparently final installment in his Batman saga, THE DARK KNIGHT RISES, is a compelling, exciting piece of modern filmmaking - more of a direct sequel to its initial entry, \\"Batman Begins,\\" than its immediate predecessor, the repetitive \\"The Dark Knight.\\"<br /><br />Set several years after the events of TDK, Gotham City finds itself once again under siege - this time from a mercenary named Bane (Tom Hardy) who wears a mask and talks like a muffled version of Darth Vader by way of Christopher Plummer in \\"Star Trek VI.\\" Nobody has seen The Batman since the night of Harvey Dent's death, with Bruce Wayne (Christian Bale, seeming less uptight this time around) hiding out as a recluse in Wayne Manor, much to the chagrin of Alfred (Michael Caine), who just wants to see his master join the ranks of the living again. Bruce's world is initially perked up by a cat burglar named Selina Kyle (Anne Hathaway), who's gotten herself wrapped up in the new world order that Bane's \\"Occupy Gotham\\" army represents - but it's just a teaser for the resurrection of Batman himself, who's desperately needed by its residents and a beleaguered Commissioner Gordon (Gary Oldman), who finds a new ally in crusading young cop John Blake (Joseph Gordon-Levitt).<br /><br />\\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" is confident, visually compelling and just a bit overlong. The script, by Nolan and his brother Jonathan, is better balanced than the redundant structure of \\"The Dark Knight,\\" which found Batman continuously trying to save Gotham, only to be thwarted by a psychotic act from The Joker. This picture is less sadistic as well, with more of a redemptive finale that proves to be enormously satisfying as Nolan at last breaks out of the constant doom and gloom that the last picture was dominated by.<br /><br />Most of the additions to the cast help enormously, from Hathaway's fetching turn as Catwoman and Gordon-Levitt's tireless police detective, to welcome on-screen appearances by veteran actors Matthew Modine and William Devane, and a few surprise cameos from veterans of Nolan's earlier Batman pictures. Bane himself is a little goofy, but Hardy packs a muscular presence, and series veterans Caine and Oldman have several fine scenes as well. Less effective is Marion Cotillard's red herring role as the new head of Wayne Enterprises and Bruce's latest love conquest - given where the story ultimately concludes, there should have been more of the relationship between Bruce and Selina, though romanticism has never been one of Nolan's strong suits.<br /><br />A few elements also fall short - a somewhat bloated stretch where Bruce finds himself in Bane's prison drags the film down with Batman sidelined for too long a duration (ironic that, no matter if it's Burton, Schumacher or Nolan, the focus always seems to drift off the Caped Crusader in every one of these pictures). There's a bit too much Bane and several pretentious, accompanying monologues, while Hans Zimmer's ridiculously over-scored soundtrack feels the need to punctuate every scene - even the quieter dramatic beats - like a sledgehammer.<br /><br />Still, it's hard to envision fans and casual viewers alike not being satisfied with this rousing third installment. Speaking of which, though billed as the concluding entry in Nolan's trilogy, it also doesn't feel like the end, unless the director is truly content in ending his series with some light finally infiltrating the darkness.

Both the LP's I received were very cleanly pressed and had no popping or tracking problems. I would go as far as saying that I consider these 120 gram pressings sound a lot better than most of the 180 gram re-issues around at the moment. This is due in no small way to the care and attention to detail that Sony has applied to every aspect of this product. Or maybe it's because the only other copies of this album I've ever seen were the battered and tattered treasure of a hard living loon. Now to the music.<br />The original Bowie mix of this album is often derided or scoffed at by fans and critics alike, however, it is the version I grew up listening to and learned to love for all of its so called flaws. The most common of these complaints are the tinny sound and the absence of bass. The remastered version is still trebly and not very bassy but has a much better separation of the instruments than the original vinyl pressing. So if you are familiar with this album you will not be disappointed it still is the Raw Power we know and love. If you aren't familiar with the album you may prefer the more standard rock mix of the album by Iggy. Either way it's a win win situation because the songs of course are great

0765316994|5.0|The problem with many science fiction stories are that they're loaded with cliches and overused plot devices.  The excellence of John Scalzi's Redshirts can be attributed to the fact that it's loaded with cliches and overused plot devices.  It clearly demonstrates that the difference between success and failure in storytelling has less to do with the material and more with the execution.<br /><br />Redshirts tells the story of the starship Intrepid which goes from dangerous mission to dangerous mission.  The principal officers always survive - usually saved by some solution seemingly pulled out of thin air - but at least one lesser crew member dies.  These crew members are Redshirts:  not because of the color of their uniforms, but because, like the hapless and doomed throwaway characters on Star Trek (who typically were clad in red), they are killed seemingly to make every mission more meaningful.<br /><br />The hero of Redshirts is ensign Andrew Dahl who soon realizes that he is a redshirt.  Veteran crew members know better than to go on away missions, but Dahl has yet to learn this fact.  And the high fatality rate among crew members is only oneof the mysteries of the Intrepid.  There's the apparently death-proof main officers, including the Captain and the Science Officer and the incredible healing ability of another officer.  There's the mysterious box that provides miraculous cures.  And more. Dahl will try to figure out what's going on and how to stop it.  An absurd problem requires an absurd solution which he will have to implement.<br /><br />To reveal what's going on would spoil the fun, and even when you think you understand, Scalzi throws in another twist or two.  Thematically, Scalzi wants to remind the readers that even the minor characters in stories are people not to be disregarded lightly.  In this vein, in addition to the novel, there are three \\"codas\\" that tells the stories of minor characters within the main story.  This is a fun and funny tale,though it would be best appreciated by science fiction fans; to others, it might seem too inside-jokey.

0316017930|3.0|The other reviews did justice to the overly anecdotal nature of Gladwell's 2008 bestseller, so I'll just make a very fine point regarding education.  In line with his overarching theme and hypothesis that success is due less to intrinsic factors (i.e. personal factors like \\"IQ\\", personality, etc.), and more to extrinsic ones (i.e. environmental factors like family upbringing, sociocultural milieu, chance encounters, etc.), Gladwell talks about how academic success is less obvious than we would otherwise think.  He cites research that divides students into three different groups of Low, Middle, and High SES, and then shows the unexpected: High SES children make academic gains in the summer (as measured by performance on the California Achievement Test), while Low SES children show evidence of regression.  Compare that with the fact that their respective gains throughout the September-June school-year are more or less comparable, and you have a case for arguing that summer vacations, if not purposeful (read: fraught with packed schedules), are counterproductive towards continued improvement in school.  Gladwell then argues for a \\"rice-paddy\\" approach to schooling-- have no fallow year, no time of un-productivity, and be like the Koreans or Japanese, who send their children to school 220+ days a year as opposed to our lesser 180, and you'll see a transformation of the education system.<br /><br />Such whimsical extrapolations are frustratingly common in the book.  For example, there is the argument that learning Asian languages, with the regularity and logic of their counting systems, makes math easier for Asian children to digest, which makes it more interesting to them, which makes them work harder, a virtuous cycle which continues until these children are significantly more math-savvy than their American counterparts; what should be made, then, of Asian-Americans, the vast majority of whom do NOT know their \\"mother tongue\\", who still lead the nation in math proficiency levels?  Or would this then be due to the cultural heritage of \\"hard work = success\\" they have inherited from their parents, as well as the comparative privilege they are born into?  It should be pointed out, though that Gladwell's original argument seems to break down when you consider that in Japanese, you actually have TWO counting systems-- the native Japanese, as well as the Chinese-derived systems, which are used in different circumstances and for different purposes, and whose readings can sometimes both apply to the same word, which complicates things not insignificantly.<br /><br />Gladwell seems to be making the (obvious) point that context is hugely important, and it is interesting here to also note that the context of Japanese schoolchildren, if explored a bit more, would perhaps reveal why they are more likely to attain greater academic achievement.  Consider the lower birthrate in Japan and what that means for the attention the Japanese schoolchildren receive.  The number of Japanese women who stay at home and consider child-rearing (and often of only children) their raison d'etre.  The shrinking population and the Japanese government's realization that this current generation must at all costs be cultivated, lest they turn out to be the likes of the so-called \\"Freeters\\", that population of no-longer adolescent but not yet adult that can't or won't hold a fulltime job, start their own families, and do as the other mainliner Japanese do.  Of course context is important.  But we've always known that, haven't we, and for every one or two details that emerge in Person A's life to set him up for success, there are perhaps hundreds others that we aren't aware of, and so is it not foolhardy to make the sorts of extrapolations that are not uncommonly made in this book?  Morever, for every Person A out there who does capitalize on those helps of parentage or patronage, what about the many other Person Bs who don't, and squander the opportunities held out soliberally to them?<br /><br />The problem of this book is that it oversimplifies things, makes success and failure seem as easily explainable as the sum of factors A + B + C.  For one, failure is far more complicated-- the Irish Potato Famine, for example, didn't just arise from a potato blight-- there was also the exploitative landowning system, GB's leeching of Irish produce during such a crucial time, etc.  In the same way, American children aren't underperforming in math and other academic subjects simply because they have summers off, or because the language of math in English is nonregular and confusing.  There are a host of other factors out there that would take a full library and research team to expound upon-- e.g. American family structures, shifts in the definition of \\"intelligence\\" in this Information Age, competitors for children's time like television watching or Internet use, and so on and so forth.<br /><br />A fun read, though, for some fun anecdotes that you can call to mind while walking here and there.  And it does make you more grateful for those seen and unseen, thanked and unthanked in your life, who were so instrumental in whatever it is that you have accomplished thus far.

B005EM8O8O|4.0|Robert Harris, the writer of The Ghost, has produced yet another thoroughly entertaining work, but one laced with a thread of anxiety.<br /><br />It's surprising how someone who apparently spent his entire career as a journalist and writer has managed to capture the atmospherics of modern-day financial trading.  And in the manner of Tom Wolfe, Harris has taken real-life events and real people and thrown them into a sort of fictionalizing cuisinart to produce a riveting tale that touches (lightly and with a chuckle) on some of the major issues of the day: e.g., Evolution, Global Conspiracy and Finance.<br /><br />The structure of the story is premised on the Flash Crash of several years ago, when the Dow Jones suddenly dropped nearly a thousand points in about a minute, in a &#34;Flash&#34;.<br /><br />But Harris, of course, is having one on a bit.<br /><br />The premise has the following contours.  The regular Joes and Josephines (?) of the world react with FEAR when confronted with the accelerating potential of Man-Made technology, Man-Made social policies and Man-Made conflicts to destroy Man and Woman and Children and, basically, everybody.  The Fear Index, the financial algorithm at the heart of the plot-theme of this story, exploits precisely that irrational quality.  The fears spawned in the endless stream of imminent apocalypses advertised in the boldface headlines of Flipboard and Pulse on the issues of the Perpetual War, Climate Change and Food Insecurity provide the algorithm with an opening.  A subtle imbalance arises in the collective unconscious that the algorithm exploits like a Hapkido master.  And so, there we go, tumbling through the air towards our silly and somewhat embarrassing demise.  The algo goes short everything, but then u-turns to buy it all up at the bottom.<br /><br />The fall is painful. Recovery is far from guaranteed.<br /><br />Taking this entertainment seriously for a moment, the implication of course is that panic is worse than techno-disaster.  The Fear Index is simply the tool that we have used to destroy ourselves.  Harris seemingly whispers that cold British calculus would have been preferable to our ancient mammalian and somewhat plebeian tendency to wring our hands and sweat bullets at the prospect of Y2K, pole shift, aliens, 2012, Mayan Prophecy, Bilderberg and the second shooter from the grassy knoll in Dallas.<br /><br />Remain calm.  Help is on the way.  All we have to fear is fear itself.  Forget this nonsense about conspiracy and the possibility of baroque and hidden powers.<br /><br />But what if there are real risks embedded in our technologies?  What if our confidence in the future really is mis-placed?  What if our fearsespecially if they are collectiveare indeed an early warning that what is amiss now is simply the start of an accelerating and very slippery slope?<br /><br />It's possible then that the Fear Index algorithm wins either way, whether quickly with our help or over the long haul all on its own, propelled only by a hidden force.<br /><br />After a few sips of wine, one comes away with the conspiratorial thought that conceiving of Man as fearful cattle is both possible and very, very effective.  So is this then how our friends at the Rand Corporation conceive of us?  (Of course, if there were something like a Fear Index algorithm, they will have written a memorandum or white paper on the topic already at Rand or Tavistock or the Max Planck Institute.)  Social Engineering is apparently possible now with a granularity and precision that was fanciful a century ago.  If I were a conspirator and social tinkerer I would, I think, very much prefer to be left alone, to tinker as I please.<br /><br />I can well appreciate that from Harris' position as a member of the Royal Society of Literary Arts that the problems at the core of Fear Index may seem like little baubles and play things.  So what that we are at the threshold of new technologies that will once again split man apart, aristocrats on one side, peasants on the other, technocrats over here and the great unwashed over there as far away as possible?<br /><br />The world will become TIDY once more.  Why be afraid?  Sounds like fun with Doomsday, because for someone out there one can be sure that Doom is just another word for Victory.<br /><br />Fear Index closes with the sort of pyrotechnics that we are used to now from Dan Brown and his stories.  The technology that threatened to eat us alive is...uh... dealt with adequately. (No spoiler there.  We all knew where it was going to end.)  All is well again, sort of.<br /><br />But I am afraid that all is not well again.  In case you have forgotten, George Orwell and Aldous Huxley also wrote tales about the victory of technology.  We are NEARER now.  The Fear Index is far more entertaining than either 1984 or Brave New World, and that ironically might be a somewhat frightening observation.  I very much enjoyed reading this book.  The unease came to me very much later.d the great unwashed over there as far away as possible?    The world will become TIDY once more.  Why be afraid? 1401235166|5.0|Well over a hundred issues and FABLES is still going strong. FABLES Vol. 17: INHERIT THE WIND collects issues #108-113, as the creative team of Bill Willingham, Mark Buckingham, and Steve Leialoha, those merchants of literary addiction, keeps feeding the fiends. This trade forks over four story arcs, two of which are prominent, the other two not so much. After the epic (but sort of anti-climactic) Mister Dark saga, Willingham grants us one of those periodic breathers. That's not to say that crucial events don't take place. Things of import transpire like a mother.<br /><br />With Bigby Wolf's father having sacrified himself in a death duel against the frightening Mister Dark, there is immediate, desperate need to fill the void he'd left as the North Wind, one of the four eminent Cardinal Winds. The only worthy candidates are Bigby and Snow White's six (but, really, seven) cub children. In the remote keep of the mountain king, the anxious parents can only look on as the former North Wind's servants impose a series of tests and trials on their little darlings to determine who is the true heir. Complications abound when the three other Cardinal Winds appear, having sworn to sink all efforts to appoint a new North Wind from the old bloodline.<br /><br />I tend to walk around grinning all day whenever the mischievous wolf cubs are showcased. I find Bigby and Snow's family to be utterly charming and irresistible. Willingham lays down some terrific character beats for the kids. I love that we get a peek into the cubs' personalities, particularly that of Ambrose, Dare, and Winter. And then there's that worrisome prophecy which foretells the cubs' individual fates or, for a few, their dooms.<br /><br />We also catch up to Bufkin, de-winged blue monkey and renowned hero of the Business Office, as he's back in the land of Oz and has enlisted in a modest rebellion against the tyrannical new Emperor of Oz. As someone who has read all of Baum's Oz novels, it's pretty gratifying to see the Sawhorse and Jack Pumpkinhead involved as Bufkin's co-conspirators, even if they tend to be on the skittish side.<br /><br />Elsewhere and concurrently, the exiles of Fabletown tentatively return to upstate New York and the rural comforts of the Farm, just in time for the holidays and for Rose Red, that newest paladin of hope, to be visited by three spirits. Meanwhile, the vengeful and sorcerously slimmed-down Mrs. Spratt continues her evolution as a villainous key player. Except that, really, there's not much forward momentum to her storyline.<br /><br />Issue #113 goes the anthology route as Willingham and a series of artists tell several short stories concerning \\"In Those Days.\\" In these four brief narratives we learn, among other things, why it was that the Adversary deigned to ignore Fabletown for as long as he did. There is also a turtle on which shell lies a kingdom contained in a ceramic teacup. And there's a horny porcupine.<br /><br />Rich in character moments, narrated with flair, enveloped in sly humor, and steeped in years of complex continuity, FABLES keeps on being one of the best - if not THE best - comic book currently going. Even though this volume sets a more leisurely pace and the stories this time out aren't staged on a grand scale, I'm still gleefully flipping the pages. When will Bill Willingham run out of ideas? Does his deal with the Devil come with an expiration date? Here's hoping not. We've just had the lull. We're ready for the big, epic stuff again. 5 out of 5 stars for this one. I can't give it anything less, given that Bigby, Snow, and the cubs are featured significantly.o see the Sawhorse and Jack Pumpkinhead involved as Bufkin's co-conspirators, even if they tend to be on the skittish side.<br /><br />Elsewhere and concurrently, the exiles of Fabletown tentatively return to upstate New York and the rural comforts of the Farm, just in time for the holidays and for Rose Red, that newest paladin of hope, to be visited by three spirits. 0743277708|4.0|You don't really need me to tell you. The eight hundred or so glowing reviews on this page (just so far) are at testament to the quality and beauty of this book. But if you're really reading this review without knowing anything about the book, here's a brief summary.<br /><br />The novel follows the tale of Hazel the rabbit and his various companions as they narrowly escape dangers in an attempt to establish their own warren (think of it as a rabbit community). It's tempting to interpret the trials that the rabbits face as if they are allegorical (there are spiritual themes, as well as lessons about unnatural behavior, helping those in need, and seeking peace before war), but in the preface Adams explicitly states that he meant nothing by the book other than to tell a story about rabbits. Although immersing one's self into the world of the rabbits -- including their habits and language (Lapine) -- takes a tiny bit of getting used to, once you are there, it's absorbing and real.<br /><br />Adams' prose is astounding and the characters are vibrant and distinct. For a book with such a large cast, rarely is it hard to distinguish one rabbit from another, and the conversations, actions, and obstacles that occur are imagined and related with stunning clarity. Obviously a naturalist, Adams demonstrates a keen eye for wilderness details. The pages seem to burst with the color, smells, and sounds of a world of wild animals.<br /><br />Others describe the book as flawless, but I wouldn't go that far. In spite of their evocative beauty, Adams' descriptions of the hills, rivers, and foliage are overdone at points. I wouldn't be surprised if the book contained, by name, every single native plant that could be found in the section of England where the action occurs. Although that level of detail is admirable, it is also distracting (I've never heard of most of these plants and even after reading the book couldn't tell you what they look like) and occasionally stabs at the pace of the novel.<br /><br />That brings me to my second minor quibble with the book. Although Adams has a craftsman's eye for detail and beauty, this works against him on a few of the novel's climactic moments. The action sequences seemed disjointed and clunky to me, especially since they relied so heavily on dialogue and -- again -- Adams' extensive use of detail. Ironically, there were points where he spent a paragraph or two describing a bridge or punt, and I still had difficulty picturing them in my head. It's a case of forest versus trees. Although Adams clearly knows he has a poet's ear for prose, he seems -- in just a few places -- not to realize that sometimes too many words can confuse rather than clarify.<br /><br />These are small issues (oh, and I didn't care for the constant epigraphs, either; they add literally nothing to the story, and after a time, I just stopped reading them) in what is otherwise an astounding book, which I would gladly recommend to everyone I know, young or old, jaded or no.the novel.<br /><br />That brings me to my second minor quibble with the book. B006PTL1QM|3.0|Essentially this is a movie about Jung and the significant relationships in his life - with his forerunner and contemporary in psychoanalysis, Freud, with his rich and patient wife, and perhaps most of all with the patient with whom he falls in love and has an affair, Sabina Spielrein.  It's hard to see the Cronenberg of old who would delve into murkiness and come up with visually inventive and often shocking ideas.. here, the photography is bright and crisp, the mannerisms impeccable and the period seemingly immaculately recreated.  The pace is measured, and in the manner of the time, \\"violent arguments\\" are mere words on paper, so much of what was said being done by letter.  Indeed, it is through the letters of Freud and Jung so much is known about them, apparently.  So it's a biography, covering the pivotal career trajectory of Jung.  And yet for all the willingness to show things for how they were (Knightley has a brave and deliberate performance, which was apparently well researched, as the detail rich commentary by Cronenberg attests), is less emotionally involving, and more.. well, clinical, if you'll forgive the analogy.  The details are interesting and well observed, the history of the two men in particular is of some significance, and yet for all the fine performances it is hard to truly be engaged emotionally.  Fassbender has a wonderful performance as Jung, finding out about himself as much as about his patients at times, and Mortensen is really wonderfully understated as he puffs his pipe, and wrestles with his opinion of the man he had hoped would carry his work forward.  Their interplay during their period of friendship is arguably the movies highlight, as they ponder each others dreams..\\"Have you considered the rolling log could be your penis..?\\".  Knightley has done a great job in a difficult role, having to convince as someone seriously mentally ill at the start, but who went on to become a significant psychoanalyst in her own right.  Alas, her Russian accent, while not awful, leaves you forever reminded she is acting, when everyone else speaks perfectly normally.  It's defensible from a movie convention point of view, but distracts.<br />Taken on its own, without any preconceptions, this is a well crafted and visually beautifully conceived movie.  It's just hard sometimes to forget that with Cronenberg we so often expect something with more bite.  Without that bite,  what we are left with is a biopic that educates a little, engages somewhat less, but is so beautifully shot and acted, it's like a Merchant Ivory film with added rude words.  6/10

It is quite an investment, but it will give you a lifetime of pleasure and I recommend it very warmly.

B002QEXBGS|5.0|This production is one of three Offenbach operettas that Minkowski has conducted. All have been notable for great energy and attention to detail.<br /><br />Orpheus in the Underworld is, of course, a version of the Greek tragedy that is anything but tragic. Fundamentally it is a send-up of the original story where both Orpheus and Eurydice have grown tired of each other for various reasons and their amorous attention has been caught elsewhere. In addition to this, both Jupiter and Pluto are determined to gain Eurydice for themselves. Eventually everyone ends up in Hell which, far from being a place of torture is a place of constant partying. This is where Eurydice would prefer to remain and Orpheus too would be happier with this solution so no sorrow, except that expressed by the character of 'Public Opinion' is expressed and the whole story ends with general jubilation.<br /><br />The whole cast of this sparkling and sacrilegious production enter fully into the spirit and the racy pace is maintained throughout. The leading parts of Orpheus, Eurydice, Jupiter and Pluto are both sung and acted with great energy and musical skill and as if they were individually born for the parts they take. Dessay has a wonderful time singing effortlessly while engaging in all sorts of non-musical activities. The same can be said especially for Laurent Naouri as Jupiter. One could really go on and list all the cast but I will content myself with simply including the dancers and the choreography for constantly uplifting work in every sense! Very imaginative.<br /><br />The orchestral contribution under their conductor is played with total commitment, considerable verve and plenty of enthusiasm.<br /><br />The settings and costumes could be described as bizarre and this is where it all becomes a matter of personal taste. They are sufficiently extreme to cause pleasure, or somewhat less likely, irritation, for those with firm views. My own view is that the whole thing is supposed to be a send-up so sending up the setting is probably appropriate so long as the musical values are not compromised and crucially, this performance delivers enormously well on the music.<br /><br />So a strong recommendation from me with just a small element of caution. If you are a strong traditionalist you may not be so keen! In my opinion though, this one is an out and out winner and well worth the full 5 stars.a send-up so sending up the setting is probably appropriate so long as the musical values are not compromised and crucially, this performance delivers enormously well on the music.<br /><br />So a strong recommendation from me with just a small element of caution. 030758836X|2.0|Does the ending of this book leave you a) wishing you hadn't read it  in the first place; b) asking, \\"where's the resolution?\\" c) feeling thoroughly unsatisfied; or d) all of the above. Answer: d<br /><br /> Think of a life experience you've had that started great only to bomb out at the end. Like a promising date that was going really well--only to be ruined by a nasty argument at the end of the evening. You go to bed feeling empty, unfulfilled. That's what reading this novel was like for me. Starts great, quickly becomes a can't-put-it-down page turner, then completely fizzles at the end, leaving you unsatisfied with the novel's lack of resolution.<br /><br /> That said, I agree with most of the positive comments about the book: \\"Fiendishly clever,\\" \\"Totally engrossing,\\" Gripping and addictive.\\" \\"Well-plotted,\\" etc. Does Gillian Flynn have excellent writing chops? Does she masterfully develop psychologically complex, multidimensional characters? Present penetrating insights into the nature of modern marriage and relationships? Write pitch-perfect dialog? Absolutely. She just needs to learn how to finish. Develop resolution.<br /><br /> One thing Flynn might want to learn to use to her advantage in future novels is the \\"recency effect,\\" which simply states that the last thing you see or experience in a given situation or event is more accessible in your memory, and therefore more likely to be remembered by you then those things that occurred in the beginning or middle of the experience. Given the novel's lack of resolution and totally unsatisfying ending, the last thing I remember about the book is my feeling of utter disappointment.

B004LWZWGK|5.0|Let's get the elephant in the room out of the way first: Heath Ledger's The Joker is a hard act to follow. Impossible, some might even say. Wisely The Dark Knight Rises doesn't try to outdo itself by bringing in a bigger and more colorful villain, for if it had that inevitably would have took it down the road of self-parody (Batman Forever, Batman and Robin anyone?). Nor does it sacrifice character in its rush to get to the next big action set-piece, the Hollywood thinking being that what audiences want from their summer blockbusters is lots of bang for their buck; after all Spider-Man 3 gave us that, and look how well that turned out.<br /><br />Make no mistake though, the scale of The Dark Knight Rises is huge: the plane hijack at the beginning, Batman's first appearance on the streets of Gotham after eight years sees him being chased by what looks like ever cop car in the city. How the film's villain, Bane, spectacularly cuts off Gotham from the outside world and traps all of its cops at the same time. Gotham's finest in blue charging Gotham's scum-of-the-earth for a Gangs of New York-style street rumble.<br /><br />But is all stays grounded, helped in no small part by the fact that Batman is one of comic's and cinema's most accessible superheroes, because he isn't a superhero; at least not in the sense that he has supernatural powers. I pointed this out to my brother when he saw Watchman and he said, \\"But there aren't any superheroes in the movie, apart from Doctor Manhattan. The rest as just vigilantes.\\" \\"Yeah,\\" I said, \\"but so is Batman.\\" You could see him thinking about it, and then the realisation dawning. Oh, Bruce Wayne's got lots of fancy James Bond-style toys to help him out, but in the end he's just a guy in a Halloween costume beating up the bad guys.<br /><br />Which leads me to one of the best sequences in The Dark Knight Rises - Bane and Batman's first fight. Other movie maker's might have gone down the road of staging some big, dump wow encounter,but Christopher Nolan brings it all down to gut level. No music. A strip of metal walkway. And two guys just slugging it out with their bare hands. Or Batman's slugging it out and Bane, for the most part, is allowing him to go at it... and thus clearly sending a message: is that it? Is that all you've got vigilante boy? An expensive costume and some boxing moves? And that's when the audience realises that Batman truly has met his match, and Christopher Nolan didn't need to bring on a big and colourful villain in order to do it: all he had to do was introduce Batman to an essentially ordinary man, but a guy who's just as determined and single-minded in achieving what he feels he has to do as Bruce Wayne is.<br /><br />And what else the movie brilliantly achieves is neatly tying its storyline into the previous two movies, to form a true unified three-part story. In fact, I would say the story development is The Dark Knight Rises' greatest achievement, in terms of how it not only shows the fallout of Harvey Dent's death but deftly weaves back on itself to echo scenes and dialogue from the previous two movies (James Gordon placing his jacket around a young Bruce Wayne's shoulders, the excellent scenes of a fallen Batman in an underground prison where the only escape route is a wide shaft leading to the hope of daylight, like the well Bruce fell down as a child ... \\"Why do we fall, Bruce?...\\"), to bringing in the League of Shadows and Ra's al Ghul. All of which culminates in the movie's brilliant sting in its tail when you discover that Bane isn't really who Batman is up against. And whilst with seeming effortlessness it does all that the movie also manages to bring in Catwoman (although she's never referred to by that name) and pair her with Batman in such a way that not only does it feel natural, but also never makes the character feel like a sidekick. At one point Batman opines to Selina Kyle/Catwoman that she's got more to give, and that's the way I've always felt about Anne Hathaway, that she's not just a chic-flick actress (as her role in Rachel Getting Married showed). She's superb as Selina Kyle/Catwoman.<br /><br />Anne Hathaway's character doesn't distract or steal the show. Indeed no one does, which is another plus in the movie's favour: all the characters contribute equally, all of them serve the story. As I've said, the movie doesn't lack for big action moments, but nor does it lack for emotional moments either. In this sense Alfred, terrifically played by Michael Caine, in many ways embodies the heart of the movie. The moment he tells Bruce that he didn't want him to come back to Gotham, that what he wanted was to go on his annual trip abroad and to sit at a table with a drink and to look up... and see Bruce sitting a few tables away, with a wife and perhaps a kid and to smile and to know that Bruce Wayne had moved on. Or the moment Alfred tells Bruce about Rachel's letter. There are just enough of these moments between various characters to not only pace the movie between its action sequences but to give it emotional weight.<br /><br />Simply put, Christopher Nolan is the best thing to ever happen to the summer blockbuster. Like film critic Mark Kermode pointed out in his superb book `The Good, the Bad and the Multiplex: What's wrong with Modern Movies?' summer blockbusters don't have to be big and dump like a Michael Bay Transformers movie, they can be big and smart and still be hugely successful at the box office, as both The Dark Knight and Inception proved.<br /><br />We all know this isn't the end: Batman is too lucrative a franchise for Warner Bros. to abandon... although pity the poor sod who's eventually tasked with a reboot after Christopher Nolan's trilogy! (And, hey, how amazing would it be if years hence Nolan and the gang got back together and did an adaptation of Frank Miller's The Dark Knight Returns? ... now THAT would be the ultimate summer blockbuster!) But for the moment -<br /><br />The legend ends -<br /><br />- and the quality of the summer blockbuster rises (well, at least until Michael Bay comes along with another Transformers movie and promptly flattens it again!).

The chorus is extremely catchy and the lyrical content is of most interest. The pre-chorus is just brilliant, it brings out so much emotion, it's basically the only part with screaming. The guitar riff in the chorus is brilliant. The breakdown is heavy as hell and the guitar solo is just so unique in every way. I can't say enough positive words about this song, just listen to it!<br /><br />4. Into the Mouth of Hell We March 9/10 - This song is hard to describe for me because I love it, but the opening riff decieves me. I like it but it doesn't amaze me like the other opening riffs for the other songs. I do however love the chorus and think it's one of the catchiest on the record. I love the little breakdown after the second chorus and I find that the song picks up at this point. I think Matt's vocals shine at this point and then the guitar solo of course is brillaint and original like all the others. I should've probably given this a 10/10 but I need to differiate between my absolute favorites.<br /><br />5. Throes of Perdition 10/10 - What an amazing opening riff, I love the cymbals in the background and then this epic double kick drum stuff breaks out with this amazingly heavy riff and then breaks into heavenly lyrics and another catchy riff. This is another one of my favorites. I love the chorus, the solo everything about it. It has a similar heavy pre-chorus like Down From the Sky which is a plus in my opinion. The drums on this song are probably the best on the whole album so if your a drummer, this is a pretty good challenge for you, great dynamics.<br /><br />6. Insurrection 8/10 - This is a straight up thrash metal jam, great song. This could've been a great song on The Crusade but here it fairs as one of the worst which means nothing because it's an amazing song with  a great riff and epic, pounding drums. The lyrics fall short on this song, I feel that the title of the song is forced into the lyrics. Not the best offering in that form but this is made up for witha great quiet breakdown and building into this almost black metal influenced bridge and then into this great guitar solo.<br /><br />7. The Calamity 8/10 - This another great song, great riffs, another catchy chorus and some more great drumming. I particularly love the bridge after the second chorus and the first verse. I also love the guitar solo, another original sounding solo with sweeps galore. This is better than Insurrection by a hair thanks to the epic verse.<br /><br />8. He Who Spawned The Furies 9/10 - I love the lyrics for this song, they stick out so well. I love the groove metal entrance riff and then I also enjoy a less catchy chorus that still holds an amazing sound. The harsh vocals are used sparingly on this song but they are positioned so well and I just love this song. I think you can guess the guitar solo is amazing according to the previously reviewed songs. But before that, the bridge between it and the chorus is just so heavy and beautiful. This is in my opinion the third best guitar solo behind Into the Mouth of Hell... and the song after this one.<br /><br />9. Of Prometheus and the Crucifix 10/10 - This is my favorite song the album, what an amazing song that holds everything I would want in a song. Great vocals, great lyrics, amazing riffs, great solo, the catchiest chorus on the record and pounding drums, I LOVE IT!!! The highlights of this song include chorus, guitar solo and pre-chorus. Just listen to it, this is defintly worth a spot on your ipod for life!<br /><br />10. Like Callisto To a Star in Heaven 9/10 - Another great song with a heavy opening riff with pounding drums. The lyrics are amazing on this song and the vocals are just so angry and merciless. The pre-chorus and the chorus are the obvious highlights with that deep voice pronouncing the title of the song and the catchy chorus dragging in the listener. I feel the second verse is better than the first and the guitar solo shines as well.<br /><br />11. Shogun 6/10 - This song befuddles me, it's not the length because I'm a huge progressive metal fan and I can handle length. The musicianship is amazing in this song nonetheless but I just don't feel a flow to this song. I feel that's just one of those songs saying \\"oh look what I can do with my instrument\\". It might be a highlight for some but to me it's the worst on the album. Don't get me wrong, I still like it and listen to it when I listen to the whole record consecutivly. Just shorten it and put more melody and flow.<br /><br />This is album is epic is every facet and i highly recommend to all metalheads who want great riffs and great vocals. I love it and I hope you do too. Pick up all their discography while your at it, In Waves (their new record) is also quite amazing.

You have to put far more effort into getting it right - your readership is going to be far more critical.<br /><br />If this is going to be a book about the magic of reality I would expect to see a lot of magical reality inside. A book like this IS needed but I would suggest it be bigger, more coherent and meet the expectations of a modern young readership. It should not be done by one author but should be a compilation of different specialists- including fiction writers like Salman Rushdie to help illustrate the difference between the world of the imagination and the world of science. It should be a grand project and not just another title to add to the oeuvre of an already best-selling author and public personality. Richard Dawkins is too busy to attempt something of the proportion demanded by this task on his own. Mr McKean should have no part to play in any future edition or similar work.
014303961X|2.0|Lady Chatterley's Lover by D. H. Lawrence<br /><br />Plot:<br /><br />The story concerns a young married woman, Constance (Lady Chatterley)--n&eacute;e Constance Reid--whose upper-class husband--Clifford Chatterley--has been paralyzed due to a war injury. Constance--Connie--was brought along her sister Hilda--amongst intellectuals and had lost her virginity as a teenager.  She was a well educated and free spirited woman who could hold her own in a conversation with men.<br /><br />Clifford was a a shy man, who was the younger brother--Herbert being the oldest--and was unhappy with inheriting the money and title when Herbert was killed in WWI.  He took a wife at the insistence of his father--Sir Geoffrey--and married Connie, after his sister--Emma--rejected the idea of marrying her own brother.  Clifford had a one month honeymoon with Connie, and went to war.  He survived it paralyzed from the waist down, thus unable to function sexually and fulfill his obligation of creating a heir to the family.<br /><br />In addition to Clifford's physical limitations, his emotional neglect of Constance forces distance between the couple. Clifford asks Connie to father a child with another man, hopefully from the same social class as the Chatterley's.  Connie's sexual frustration leads her into an affair with the gamekeeper, Oliver Mellors. The class difference between the couple highlights a major motif of the novel which is the unfair dominance of intellectuals over the working class.<br /><br />The novel is about Constance's realization that she cannot live with the mind alone; she must also be alive physically. This realization stems from a heightened sexual experience Constance has only felt with Mellors, proving that love can only happen with the element of the body, not the mind. thus at the end she decides to leave Clifford and marry Oliver.<br /><br />Comments:<br /><br />In Lady Chatterley's Lover, Lawrence comes full circle to argue once again for individual regeneration, which can be found only through the relationship between man and woman (and, he asserts sometimes, man and man). Love and personal relationships are the threads that bind this novel together. Lawrence explores a wide range of different types of relationships. The reader sees the brutal, bullying relationship between Mellors and his wife Bertha, who punishes him by preventing his pleasure. There is Tommy Dukes, who has no relationship because he cannot find a woman whom he respects intellectually and, at the same time, finds desirable. There is also the perverse, maternal relationship that ultimately develops between Clifford and Mrs. Bolton, his caring nurse, after Connie has left.<br /><br />It is a slow read, bordering on the boring....ch can be found only through the relationship between man and woman (and, he asserts sometimes, man and man). Screenplay - how the concept of the films was born with Nolan & David Goyer, writing the scripts, and taking inspiration from the comics when crafting the story.<br /><br />2. Production Design - building the sets, scouting & dressing locations, designing Batman's vehicles.  Includes some concept art.<br /><br />3. Cast - casting the characters, insight into the actors' process.  One of the best aspects of the films is that Nolan assembles a very strong cast, even in smaller supporting roles.  Obviously, much of the focus is placed on selecting Christian Bale to play Bruce Wayne.<br /><br />4. Costumes & Makeup - discussion about the costumes of the film, including plenty of costume sketches.  Includes detail about the evolution of Batman's suit, the Joker's makeup, Bane's mask, etc.<br /><br />5. The Shoot: Batman Begins - a chronicle of the 129-day shoot, Nolan's directing style, filming on location, shooting the Tumbler chase, etc,<br /><br />6. The Shoot: The Dark Knight - filming in Chicago, IMAX cameras, the hospital explosion, etc.<br /><br />7. The Shoot: The Dark Knight Rises - filming in Pittsburgh and India, shooting the opening plane sequence practically, destroying a football stadium, etc.<br /><br />8. Special Effects & Stunts - insight into the stunt work with effects coordinator Chris Corbould. Editing, Music, and Sound - Lee Smith on editing the films, and specifically how be cut effectively between parallel story lines and action.  Scoring the film, including quotes from composers Hans Zimmer and James Newton Howard. Visual Effects - It seems like these films used every method of visual effects available, from models and miniatures to computer-generated effects.  Specific topics include creating digital bats and the monorail crash in Batman Begins, the ferry boat sequence from The Dark Knight, and the stadium explosions in The Dark Knight Rises.<br /><br />11.
